  <span id="freeText6317540552548971" style="display:none">if you dont read this book youre a fucking idiot. im serious.<br /><br />i think the ONLY thing fanon was even slightly wrong about was pan africanism and the relationship black americans (in north and south america--forgive me, im brazilian after all) have with racism. i think he dismisses the level of violence they (we? if i may include mixed race black people here) in the americas have and continue to endure since the time this book was written.<br /><br />that being said, this is a masterpiece. fanon knew he was dying. he wrote it, dictated many parts of it to his wife, shortly before he died of leukemia. he is a man who DESPERATELY wants you to listen. sure, you should read this if you are white, but he is speaking to us, to those of us in the third world, those of us who have something to lose, but most of all, to those of us with absolutely nothing to lose. the wretched of the earth is a call to action, but to me, it is most importantly a love letter to the third world. we cannot act if we do not love ourselves first. and we cannot think to love ourselves if we do not move towards liberation.<br /><br />in order to love ourselves, we must liberate ourselves.<br /><br />you need to read this book. its probably the most important work you will ever read in your life.</span>

<div id="review_29397562" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/29397562" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/263205-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442500731p2/263205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/29397562?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/263205-matthew">Matthew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer29397562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7015455432674460514">Like Fanon's previous (and, from my perspective, better) work <i>Black Skin, White Masks</i>, as a middle class caucasian male (MC^2, if you will) it's difficult to offer a critique to <i>The Wretched of the Earth</i> that feels either relevant or responsible. After all, in Fanon's terms, I am (at least through complacency) part of the problem that this work tries to solve: writing this review is a bit like a 1950's Republican critiquing <i>The Feminine Mystique</i>. (Is there really anything to learn here, apart fr</span>
  <span id="freeText7015455432674460514" style="display:none">Like Fanon's previous (and, from my perspective, better) work <i>Black Skin, White Masks</i>, as a middle class caucasian male (MC^2, if you will) it's difficult to offer a critique to <i>The Wretched of the Earth</i> that feels either relevant or responsible. After all, in Fanon's terms, I am (at least through complacency) part of the problem that this work tries to solve: writing this review is a bit like a 1950's Republican critiquing <i>The Feminine Mystique</i>. (Is there really anything to learn here, apart from what form the self-justification will take?)<br /><br />Of course, at least in part, a strength of this book comes exactly from its unwillingness to concede anything, to concede any kind of legitimate authority -- even as a reader -- to colonialism, and the citizens of colonial powers. Uncompromising: it's a word that's thrown around a lot, but in this case really fits. There are echoes of Marx here, and Sun Tzu and Machiavelli, but these influences are really incidental as with chilling logic, and singleminded purpose, Fanon discusses the need to oppose colonialism through armed resistance and the challenges facing nascent post-colonial states. His agenda is pragmatic, not theoretical; specific to a time and place, rather than aspiring to the universal. This book is a psychological how-to manual for insurgency and revolutionary warfare.<br /><br />Although it's difficult to find counter-arguments to Fanon's conclusion that armed revolt is essential to ending colonialism, it's also difficult (at least, sitting here in my comfortable middle class apartment) to approve of his conclusions. Easy to say, Fanon would argue, and also the predictable response of the established colonial power. True, but in the clarity of historical hindsight it also seems apparent that -- in most cases -- violence only breeds further violence, and that the most successful revolutions, like those of Gandhi and MLK, were the (relatively) bloodless ones.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7015455432674460514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_154361210" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154361210" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/442654-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513093267p2/442654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/154361210?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/442654-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=theeeeeeory">theeeeeeory</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=postcolonial-theory">postcolonial-theory</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer154361210" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4036890810549571658">Frantz Fanon was considered a radical thinker in his time. But nowadays, who honestly defends colonization? Maybe a few old right wing French and Brits muttering through their mustaches in smoke-filled bars in Aix-en-Provence and Sheffield, but the rest of us have come to realize the truth of the matter. In "Black Skin, White Masks," Fanon performed a subtle psychological analysis of the colonial situation, but in "The Wretched of the Earth," he takes his fight to the streets. And he has plenty </span>
  <span id="freeText4036890810549571658" style="display:none">Frantz Fanon was considered a radical thinker in his time. But nowadays, who honestly defends colonization? Maybe a few old right wing French and Brits muttering through their mustaches in smoke-filled bars in Aix-en-Provence and Sheffield, but the rest of us have come to realize the truth of the matter. In "Black Skin, White Masks," Fanon performed a subtle psychological analysis of the colonial situation, but in "The Wretched of the Earth," he takes his fight to the streets. And he has plenty of horror stories from his work as a clinical psychiatrist in Algeria to back it up.<br /><br />Fanon, also, unlike some of his acolytes, approached the postcolonial situation with a certain degree of skepticism. He recognized the follies of tribalism, and the vicious economic imperialism being conducted by French and American corporations in the newly liberated nations of Africa, the Arab World, and Asia. And, much like the Senegalese filmmaker Ousmane Sembène, he demonstrates how the vulgar nouveaux-riches of those nations are just as much the enemy of the people of the third world as the colonists. While Fanon's analysis is a bit dated now, the heart of the matter remains the same, and this is still very relevant reading for anyone interested in the developing world.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4036890810549571658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer331835623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9708199032113854209">This is <i>the</i> book to read to understand the exploitative relationship between the colonizers and the colonized and is a damning critique on the history of colonialism as an institution(particularly in the French-Algerian context). It is a blend of anthropology, sociology, philosophy and psychology (Fanon's roots were in medicine, and particularly psychiatry, after all, and we can sense an indebtedness here to the writings of Freud, whom Fanon cites in the text). Parts of it seemed also to draw on</span>
  <span id="freeText9708199032113854209" style="display:none">This is <i>the</i> book to read to understand the exploitative relationship between the colonizers and the colonized and is a damning critique on the history of colonialism as an institution(particularly in the French-Algerian context). It is a blend of anthropology, sociology, philosophy and psychology (Fanon's roots were in medicine, and particularly psychiatry, after all, and we can sense an indebtedness here to the writings of Freud, whom Fanon cites in the text). Parts of it seemed also to draw on Sartre's <i>Anti-Semite and Jew,</i> particularly Fanon's discussion of how colonialism can lead to a denial of (or loss of) the self. It is fitting in this sense, not just because of both of their ties to the Left and to Marxism, but because of the overlapping of their research/philosophical interests that Sartre wrote the preface for this book in 1961.<br /><br />Sartre's preface, though chastised by Homi Bhabha in his foreword and criticized sharply by many, notably Hannah Arendt, for its focus on violence, is very engaging and certainly vitriolic. The great thinker on free will and humanism that he was, Sartre condemns the system, the colonizers in Europe, "that super-European monster, North America," and the hypocritical liberals (we are all part of the problem, but we can also be part of the solution). He writes "Have the courage to read it, primarily because it will make you feel ashamed, and shame, as Marx said, is a revolutionary feeling." It is with a sense of shame that we encounter this work, as Sartre says, but it also leaves us with a sense of hope that things can be different, a sentiment Sartre expounds later on, but also just a few sentences later when he writes, "I, a European, am stealing my enemy's book and turning it into a way of healing Europe." <br /><br />When reading any translation, so much can be lost. And I don't know if it was for this reason or just due to more engaging styles of writing that I found greater satisfaction in the sections by Homi Bhabha, Jean-Paul Sartre and the translator Richard Philcox (then there is the fact that this book was put together when Fanon was in a fever, halfway between life and death -- these his final thoughts, important, but perhaps not quite as organized as they could have been given different circumstances; but this is only suppositional). I enjoyed Richard Philcox's discussion of the translation itself, where he states that he was trying to make Fanon relevant again for a new generation, "bringing a dead translation back to life." I think both he and Homi Bhabha (in the foreword) make strong cases for the continued relevancy of Fanon in our world today, the former pointing to the conflicts in the Middle East, the latter pointing to the effects of globalization. The main work itself, I would probably give 4 stars -- it is an explosive and engaging work, filled with Fanon's righteous indignation and intriguing thoughts -- but it is elevated by the writings that accompany it. Fanon needs no crutches. He stands well enough on his own. But the accompanying pieces add to the whole, like that certain piece of furniture in an already well-decorated room. <br /><br />None of this is intended to minimize the importance of what Fanon has to say. His critiques of dualism, his reflections on art in colonial society, his psychological case studies, his thoughts on violence, are all very intriguing and must be read, for though the colonial system may have been dismantled, the problems he discusses are still with us today. After reading Fanon it is easy to see why the <i>New York Review of Books</i> called him a "Black Rousseau . . . Jacobin in method, Rousseauist in spirit, and Sartrian in language." I would add that a comparison to Thomas Paine would not be unjustified. But Fanon, too, is much more than this. Reading Fanon can cause us a sense of shame, but it is in understanding the problems that we can create a new future that recognizes the humanity and dignity deserved by all. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9708199032113854209" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8437827327208764816" style="display:none">Dr. Franz Fanon wrote The Wretched of the Earth in 1961; it was the same year that he died, it was the same year that I was born. And while there is nothing significant in the last fact to anyone beyond myself, I find myself chagrined to note that I've been on this earth 55 years without previously encountering Fanon's work. <br /><br />The Wretched of the Earth is an important book. It was in 1961 and I believe it remains so in 2016. Fanon's analysis of the necessity for violence as the primary means to truly liberate colonized people (specifically Black-Arab Africans of Algeria) culturally, economically, nationally and psychologically will challenge the thinking of many living in an era which experienced Dr. King Jr.'s non-violent civil rights movement. Reading with an open mind to the parallels between colonial Algeria and the U.S. in the present day one can more easily understand the context spontaneous #BlackLivesMatter movement. <br /><br />Dr. Fanon is indeed a physician; a psychiatrist. His chapter on the colonial war and its relationship to mental disorders in both the colonized and colonizing peoples, complete with case studies is a fascinating look at how living under a colonizing, despotic regime is indeed crazy-making. Yet in 1961, Dr. Fanon expresses cautious hope that the nations of the African continent will reject the European model and lifestyle; will create its own way of thinking, its own unique humanity.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer429696927928222042">A masterpiece in its imagination, scholarship, and theory, Frantz Fanon's "The Wretched of the Earth" lays the groundwork for much of racial theory that would follow.<br/><br/>In succinct yet thorough chapters Fanon lays out the ways in which a colonized people can gain its independence from Europe and embark on a journey to independence, avoiding landmines on the way. And in each step, Fanon's radical method is simple: understand that the masses hold the keys to the kingdom, pre-colonial history should b</span>
  <span id="freeText429696927928222042" style="display:none">A masterpiece in its imagination, scholarship, and theory, Frantz Fanon's "The Wretched of the Earth" lays the groundwork for much of racial theory that would follow.<br /><br />In succinct yet thorough chapters Fanon lays out the ways in which a colonized people can gain its independence from Europe and embark on a journey to independence, avoiding landmines on the way. And in each step, Fanon's radical method is simple: understand that the masses hold the keys to the kingdom, pre-colonial history should be recognized but not reified, and national culture can encourage political education and help combat the pseudo-bourgeoisie and nationalism that may crop up in the newly independent state.<br /><br />The last chapter, on psychoanalysis and the colonial subject, is out of place, but the rest of the book is so remarkable that it earned 5 stars despite the mistake of including it. In a time where racial ideas are being challenged, brought into the global mindset, and discussed openly, Fanon's book reminds of the importance of saying "no" to concessions and demanding the radical changes that will ensure a better society for all.</span>

<div id="review_113351911" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/113351911" />
    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1720891-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/113351911?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane" href="/user/show/1720891-jane">Jane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1720891-jane?shelf=academic-includes-partial">academic-includes-partial</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer113351911" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18200503809503543313">I recommend Colin's review, especially on Fanon's masculinist approach - go read that.<br/><br/>Other things to say - I think "On Violence" is the best essay in the book, and put in front for a reason. It's brilliantly written, an unforgettable indictment of the utter, unforgivable evil of colonialism. I won't forget Fanon's characterization of Nazi Germany's empire as colonialism within Europe, which I think is incredibly illuminating. <br/><br/>That said, I hate the way discussions of "On Violence" seem to go. Pe</span>
  <span id="freeText18200503809503543313" style="display:none">I recommend Colin's review, especially on Fanon's masculinist approach - go read that.<br /><br />Other things to say - I think "On Violence" is the best essay in the book, and put in front for a reason. It's brilliantly written, an unforgettable indictment of the utter, unforgivable evil of colonialism. I won't forget Fanon's characterization of Nazi Germany's empire as colonialism within Europe, which I think is incredibly illuminating. <br /><br />That said, I hate the way discussions of "On Violence" seem to go. People often frame the discussion of violence in terms of "inevitability" or "justification." But what are the costs of anti-colonialist violence to the colonized people? What are the complexities of the colonized society? Is the colonized great-grandmother a less typical or worthy subject to consider than the young, abled, straight, colonized man? Fanon was a humanist. Shouldn't humanists seriously consider the merits of hiding in the basement? I'm not talking about what's justified - I'm talking about people making rational, non-mystical choices about what's best for them and theirs. Did the Warsaw rebels really make a better choice than moms who tried to stay with their kids as long as they could, even to the camps and death?<br /><br />There's an impulse to say, well, this is a historical document. But terrorism, important in the Algerian struggle, is far from dead. The strategies Fanon is talking about are still with us. What attitude will we take to them? Are colonized (or formerly colonized) people well served by them? If you succeed in killing a few hundred, or a few thousand, of the enemy (and that's granting the premise of "the enemy," which will not always be the case - that is, you're likely to kill other people who are also victims of colonialism), is that a victory? Does it advance your cause, make you or your people safer, healthier, more prosperous, more free, happier, more sane? Such questions should not be buried in a flurry of mystification, as if they were heretical. <br /><br />To speak of "inevitability" is to treat colonized people as machines, and to erase all differences among them and among the strategies they have adopted.<br /><br />To bury reasonable questions in mystification, to make them unthinkable, is the lingering method of cadre-group-style Marxism, whose discursive mode is still with us and mars Fanon's book. It also seeks to deflect our attention from the costs and crimes of communist states. Let's not celebrate the great revolutionary victories of Fanon's era till we've finished counting the dead.<br /><br />And yet - 4 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18200503809503543313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1815173073" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1815173073" />
    <a title="Kaelan Ratcliffe ▪ كايِلان راتكِليف" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe"><img alt="Kaelan Ratcliffe ▪ كايِلان راتكِليف" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585082951p2/33500045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1815173073?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaelan Ratcliffe ▪ كايِلان راتكِليف" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaelan Ratcliffe ▪ كايِلان راتكِليف" href="/user/show/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe">Kaelan Ratcliffe ▪ كايِلان راتكِليف</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141186542" href="/book/show/822730.The_Wretched_of_the_Earth">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe?shelf=africa">africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe?shelf=radical-history">radical-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe?shelf=civilisation-critique-of">civilisation-critique-of</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe?shelf=colonialism-imperialism">colonialism-imperialism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe?shelf=class">class</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe?shelf=indigenous-peoples">indigenous-peoples</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33500045-kaelan-ratcliffe?shelf=political-science">political-science</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1815173073" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10263276063432718148"><b>
  <u>Concerning Violence</u>
</b><br/><br/>There's a fascinating blurb on the back of my copy of this book. It reads: <br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>Many of the great calls to arms from the era of decolonization are now purely of historical interest, yet this passionate analysis of the relations between the great powers and the Third World is just as illuminating about the world we live in today.</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>This reminded me of something Noam Chomsky mentions about how free societies may allow dissident voices to exist, but will do anything to sideline o</span>
  <span id="freeText10263276063432718148" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>Concerning Violence</u>
</b><br /><br />There's a fascinating blurb on the back of my copy of this book. It reads: <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Many of the great calls to arms from the era of decolonization are now purely of historical interest, yet this passionate analysis of the relations between the great powers and the Third World is just as illuminating about the world we live in today.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />This reminded me of something Noam Chomsky mentions about how free societies may allow dissident voices to exist, but will do anything to sideline or marginalise those voices. In this case, this book is allowed to exist - even published by a large publisher like Penguin - but it must be de-fanged. By categorising its content as "of historical interest" it relegates anything Fanon has to say about violence in response to oppression, decolonisation, as well as colonisation, as something that has finished and is no longer in occurrence. Completely false. <br /><br />As for the book itself, I struggled quite immensely with Fanons prose. However, I think a large part of this came from the innate understanding that this book simply isn't written for me, it's written for those who have been under boot of oppression for decades. Fanon was writing in a similar vein to other political commentators when faced with a new political phenomenon. Hannah Arendt was similar in her almost post-modernistic style of writing when trying to describe Totalitarianism. Fanon, in these pages, equally attempts to describe and explain what happens on an economic, political, spiritual, cultural and psychological level to countries in the midst of - or recovering from - overt and direct colonisation. As such, his style of writing is a mix between an author telling a story, and a political scientist describing worldly events in a removed fashion. I would warn readers that if you commit to this relatively short book; then COMMIT. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10263276063432718148" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_50862222" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50862222" />
    <a title="Natalie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/181878-natalie"><img alt="Natalie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1192894391p2/181878.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50862222?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natalie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natalie" href="/user/show/181878-natalie">Natalie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/181878-natalie?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/181878-natalie?shelf=global-studies">global-studies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/181878-natalie?shelf=revolution">revolution</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50862222" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15512481944952405370">This is one of those books that I am embarrassed to lend out to other people because I have underlined in it EVERYWHERE. <br/><br/>The first 60 or so pages were very slow for me, but then Fanon really hit gold. As Sartre pointed out in his introduction, I was not the audience for this book. It was written for his revolutionary compatriots in the 3rd World. And it was stirring. <br/><br/>This rating would be 4.5 stars if available. The subject matter was incredibly important. You could tell it occurred to him at cer</span>
  <span id="freeText15512481944952405370" style="display:none">This is one of those books that I am embarrassed to lend out to other people because I have underlined in it EVERYWHERE. <br /><br />The first 60 or so pages were very slow for me, but then Fanon really hit gold. As Sartre pointed out in his introduction, I was not the audience for this book. It was written for his revolutionary compatriots in the 3rd World. And it was stirring. <br /><br />This rating would be 4.5 stars if available. The subject matter was incredibly important. You could tell it occurred to him at certain moments that maybe he should be using gender inclusive language, but he didn't bother to try for the majority of the text. Some of the section on culture and intellectualism fell flat for me as well. But the good parts - WOWZA!<br /><br />This is a must-read for his discussion about violence, his analysis of the pitfalls that can trap newly-independent nations, and his heart-wrenching description of the mental disorders brought on by colonialism and the fight against it. The conclusion was pretty incredible as well, one of the strongest indictments I've ever read of the Western model of society. <br /><br />Sartre's intro was interesting as well - his notion of violence as a boomerang was particularly compelling. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15512481944952405370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2798459495" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2798459495" />
    <a title="Vince Will Iam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/92251982-vince-will-iam"><img alt="Vince Will Iam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553632219p2/92251982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2798459495?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vince Will Iam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vince Will Iam" href="/user/show/92251982-vince-will-iam">Vince Will Iam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/92251982-vince-will-iam?shelf=post-colonial-commonwealth">post-colonial-commonwealth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/92251982-vince-will-iam?shelf=to-read-in-2020">to-read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/92251982-vince-will-iam?shelf=caribbean">caribbean</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/92251982-vince-will-iam?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2798459495" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9158093607993688767">My second book by Fanon. I was pleased to rediscover his powerful voice, one that stands out for its clarity and wisdom to defend the oppressed people. This book was written against the background of the decolonization of Africa when burgeoning African nations had to find the right path to dignity and prosperity. <br/><br/>It's worth noticing the incredible preface by Jean-Paul Sartre which really sets the tone. Fanon dissects the mechanisms of oppression and the political games which have kept young Afri</span>
  <span id="freeText9158093607993688767" style="display:none">My second book by Fanon. I was pleased to rediscover his powerful voice, one that stands out for its clarity and wisdom to defend the oppressed people. This book was written against the background of the decolonization of Africa when burgeoning African nations had to find the right path to dignity and prosperity. <br /><br />It's worth noticing the incredible preface by Jean-Paul Sartre which really sets the tone. Fanon dissects the mechanisms of oppression and the political games which have kept young African nations from developing. It is a fierce attack on Western civilization seen as morally degraded and rotting, but also on the African political elites that have too often strayed from the masses and stuffed their pockets after independence.<br /><br />I quite agree with Sartre's analysis. This book sort of holds up a mirror to Europe but was written for people of African descent and from former colonies. I'm generally not into politics but Fanon keeps it entertaining enough. A must-read if you are really interested in post-colonialism, otherwise you might be disappointed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9158093607993688767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

	<tr>
		<td>
		    <b>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('THSlXx6T8NSYGb4UE8KBgqY0irTxvTwrZdvAhpoYdRL7FgS45fgZTA4wtd5duZQy3PQ2mp67AEbCeJcr4cctkA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3I37ANyjD6iUhZUaOydSYdT6DKHPL2BGHhi7f0KBRJ5r71rnJ8jmMAKsntB1XEfRrjqwj6ApXCu5u+zSOV4cHA==')})
  }

Power is the ultimate determinant in human society, being basic to the relations within any group and bet<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6990742865106722429\" style=\"display:none\">The decisiveness of the short period of colonialism and its negative consequences for Africa spring mainly from the fact that Africa lost power. Power is the ultimate determinant in human society, being basic to the relations within any group and between groups. It implies the ability to defend one\'s interests and if necessary to impose one’s will by any means available. In relations between peoples, the question of power determines maneuverability in bargaining, the extent to which a people survive as a physical and cultural entity. When one society finds itself forced to relinquish power entirely to another society, that in itself is a form of underdevelopment.\nBefore a bomb ended his life in the summer of 1980, Walter Rodney had created a powerful legacy. This pivotal work, How Europe Underdeveloped Africa, had already brought a new perspective to the question of underdevelopment in Africa. his Marxist analysis went far beyond the heretofore accepted approach in the study of Third World underdevelopment. How Europe Underdeveloped Africa is an excellent introductory study for the student who wishes to better understand the dynamics of Africa’s contemporary relations with the West.<\/span>\n  <a Neither Jefferson nor the other Found­ers could ever have envisioned the modern national security state, with its tens of thousands of \"privateers\"; <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11409788573858175382\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;One of my favorite ideas is, never to keep an unnecessary soldier,&quot; Thomas Jefferson wrote in 1792. Neither Jefferson nor the other Found­ers could ever have envisioned the modern national security state, with its tens of thousands of &quot;privateers&quot;; its bloated Department of Homeland Security; its rust­ing nuclear weapons, ill-maintained and difficult to dismantle; and its strange fascination with an unproven counterinsurgency doctrine. Written with bracing wit and intelligence, Rachel Maddow\'s Drift argues that we\'ve drifted away from America\'s original ideals and become a nation weirdly at peace with perpetual war, with all the financial and human costs that entails. To understand how we\'ve arrived at such a dangerous place, Maddow takes us from the Vietnam War to today\'s war in Afghanistan, along the way exploring the disturbing rise of executive authority, the gradual outsourcing of our war-making capabilities to private companies, the plummeting percentage of American families whose children fight our constant wars for us, and even the changing fortunes of G.I. Joe. She offers up a fresh, unsparing appraisal of Reagan\'s radical presidency. Ultimately, she shows us just how much we stand to lose by allowing the priorities of the national security state to overpower our political discourse. Sensible yet provocative, dead serious yet seri­ously funny, Drift will reinvigorate a &quot;loud and jangly&quot; political debate about how, when, and where to apply America\'s strength and He also maps out the external and internal forces that destroyed communism, and the disastrous impact of the “free-market” victory on eastern Europe and the former Soviet Union. He affirms the relevance of taboo ideologies like Marxism, demonstrating the importance of class analysis in understanding political realities and dealing with the ongoing collision between ecology and global corporatism.\n\nWritten with lucid and compelling style, this book goes beyond truncated modes of thought, inviting us to entertain iconoclastic views, and to ask why things are as they are. It is a bold and entertaining exploration of the epic struggles of yesterday and today.\n\n&quot;A penetrating and persuasive writer with an astonishing array of documentation to implement his attacks.&quot;—The Catholic Journalist\n\n&quot;Blackshirts &amp; Reds discusses the great combat between fascism and socialism that is the defining feature of the Twentieth Century, and takes every official version to task for its substitution of moral analysis for critical analysis, for its selectivity, and for its errata. By portraying the struggle between fascism and Communism in this century as a single conflict, and not a series of discrete encounters, between the insatiable need for new capital on the one hand and the survival of a system under siege on the other, Parenti defines fascism as the weapon of capitalism, not simply an extreme form of it. Fascism is not an aberration, he points out, but a &quot;rational&quot; and integral component of the system.&quot;—Stan Goff, The Prism\n\nMichael Parenti, PhD Yale, is an internationally known author and lecturer. He is one of the nation\'s leadiing progressive political analysts. He is the author of over 275 published articles and twenty books. His writings are published in popular periodicals, scholarly journals, and his op-ed pieces have been in leading newspapers such as The New York Times and The Los Angeles Times. Davis has put the case for the latest abolition movement in American life: the abolition of the prison. As she quite correctly notes, American life is replete with abolition movements, and when they were engaged in these struggles, their chances of success seemed almost unthinkable. For generations of Americans, the abolition of slavery was sheerest illusion. Similarly,the entrenched system of racial segregation seemed to last forever, and generations lived in the midst of the practice, with few predicting its passage from custom. The brutal, exploitative (dare one say lucrative?) convict-lease system that succeeded formal slavery reaped millions to southern jurisdictions (and untold miseries for tens of thousands of men, and women). Few predicted its passing from the American penal landscape. Davis expertly argues how social movements transformed these social, political and cultural institutions, and made such practices untenable.In Are Prisons Obsolete?, Professor Davis seeks to illustrate that the time for the prison is approaching an end.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/37728.Frantz_Fanon">About Frantz Fanon</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/37728.Frantz_Fanon"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1258146314p3/37728.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/37728.Frantz_Fanon">Frantz Fanon
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,196
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16952054224358637875">Frantz Fanon was a psychiatrist, philosopher, revolutionary, and author from Martinique. He was influential in the field of post-colonial studies and was perhaps the pre-eminent thinker of the 20th century on the issue of decolonization and the psychopathology of colonization. His works have inspired anti-colonial liberation movements for more than four decades.</span>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
